    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2808384526" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2808384526" />
    <a title="Morgan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90349457-morgan"><img alt="Morgan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555380936p2/90349457.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2808384526?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Morgan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Morgan" href="/user/show/90349457-morgan">Morgan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/90349457-morgan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2808384526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18356469471031306080"><blockquote>"Je suis une sale garce, tu ne peux pas me tuer" - Phillipe Charboneau<br/>"I'm a bad bitch, you can't kill me." - Philip Kent</blockquote><br/><br/>Holy hell, my history loving soul <i>needed</i> this story.<br/><br/>This book was <b>long</b>, this book was <b>intense</b>, this book was <b>extremely well paced</b>, and this book was....well...let's just say it had that bitter after taste that sits in the mouth after consuming something <b>problematic</b>. But I'll get to that in a minute<br/><br/>First, <b>what is this book about?</b><br/><br/>The Bastard follows the story of Phillipe Charbon</span>
  <span id="freeText18356469471031306080" style="display:none"><blockquote>"Je suis une sale garce, tu ne peux pas me tuer" - Phillipe Charboneau<br>"I'm a bad bitch, you can't kill me." - Philip Kent</blockquote><br><br>Holy hell, my history loving soul <i>needed</i> this story.<br><br>This book was <b>long</b>, this book was <b>intense</b>, this book was <b>extremely well paced</b>, and this book was....well...let's just say it had that bitter after taste that sits in the mouth after consuming something <b>problematic</b>. But I'll get to that in a minute<br><br>First, <b>what is this book about?</b><br><br>The Bastard follows the story of Phillipe Charboneau, the son of a retired actress/tavern owner living in a farming village in France. All of Phillipe's life, his mother has prepared him for something greater than the life they currently lead. He doesn't find out until the age of seventeen that he is the bastard child of the English Duke of Kentland, and apparently the Duke isn't a complete pile of garbage and has promised half of his inheritance upon his death to Phillipe.<br><br>Long story short, Phillipe's mother receives word that the Duke has fallen ill and could die at any moment. So, naturally, she scoops up her son and runs off to England to make their claim to the family's fortune. And, naturally, this does not go over well.<br><br>And so begins the adventures of Phillipe as he travels from France to the countryside of England, to London, to Boston, to Concord, Philadelphia, AND MORE.<br><br>But before I get into the specifics and my overall thoughts I wanted to touch on that little sprinkle of <b>problematic content</b> real quick before it literally eats me alive.<br><br>I'm not sure where in the "Product of its Time" field this book falls in. Published in 1974 and taking place throughout the mid 1770s, there was a lot going against it. I was sent for a whirlwind almost immediately as the teenage Phillipe drags himself home after a brutal beating from his bully of a neighbor and passes out inside his family's barn. He is woken up by their kitchen maid, Charlotte, who reeks of wine and forces herself on Phillipe quite aggressively. I was....uncomfy, to say the least.<br><br>After that shady slice of sexual assault, Phillipe goes on for a record breaking amount of time of <i>only having sex outdoors and in barns</i>. I'm not kidding when I tell you that toward the end of the book when Phillipe (or Philip, as he was called at that point) finally went to consensual pound town on a <i>BED</i>, I vocally celebrated....while sitting on a crowded train on my commute home. I was very proud of him.<br><br>The majority of the rest of the problems I had tended to be within Philipe/Philip's internal thoughts. Mostly things he didn't actually act on, but still spooked me a bit.<br><br>Examples of this were his internally stated intentions that "he would bed her before the month was up." Like....aight Philip, take a breath for a second.<br><br>Or "He kept his fists at his sides and repressed his impulse to wrap his hands around her neck". Philip....GEEZ.<br><br>Now.....for the most part, he never acts on these things. He just thinks them. And actions speak louder than internalized thoughts. However, I thought it was important to at least point them out and how reading these thoughts coming from the story's hero was a bit...<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979612.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But I digress.<br><br>Also the fact that one of the African American characters was referred to as "the black" within the narrative which was a biiiiiiiit<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979613.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>OH, AND FOR THE LOVE OF GOD. This...this one scene. <br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[When Philip returns to the City Tavern in Philadelphia after learning that his father is, in fact...uhhh.....NOT DEAD, and Alicia is just chilling in his room like the dutiful wife in an early 2000s sitcom? That scene? Yeah, NO WAY.<br><br>Philip is, of course, pretty pissed that this girl he is KindOfSortOfNotReally in love with was lying right to his face about the death of his father, the Duke of Kentland. This is very awkward, considering Alicia invented porn and was posing in the corner wearing nothing but a bed sheet, hair tossed and ready for some loOoOoove making.<br><br>Ain't no love for you tonight, girl.<br><br>Annnnd ACTION on a scene that consists of angry screaming and Alicia running around crying while completely nude. I was spooked by this, mostly just because...yikes, to everything about the situation. However, it was poetic justice that she had to deal with this embarrassing confrontation considering WHAT SHE DID!<br><br>HOWEVER.<br><br>She did not deserve Philip ripping the sheet off of her and shoving his hand in between her legs to make a point about how she wouldn't be throwing herself at him if he were not now the only heir to the Amberly fortune.<br><br>I felt like this scene was equivalent to the child orgy scene in Stephen King's IT. The book is great, we are having a good time and then<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979614.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Hit the GOD DAMN BREAKS, you put your hand WHERE?<br><br>I honestly just wish I could forget it. Or just block out that paragraph like the Mueller Report.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>So.....with all those things considered.....let's move on.<br><br>I didn't find anything in the overall message of this book to be harmful in anyway and it was actually a bit informational for anyone who might not be a weirdo who knows the entire timeline of the Revolutionary War by heart, like me. So. With the aforementioned moments of uncomfiness aside, I really, <i>really</i>, enjoyed this book.<br><br>I can't comprehend how this is only 500+ pages, with the amount of events that took place and the amount of time that went by, it felt like it should have been at least 1,000+.<br><br>Phillipe/Philip traveled through three countries within this story, made sets of friends in every single one, and was beaten senseless by at least one person in every location he landed in! Amazing!<br><br>I think the most spectacular part about this book was the fact that Philip interacted with people of this time who are almost on a godlike level in American history today and every interaction felt natural.<br><br>It didn't matter the name, Franklin, Lafayette, Adams, Revere, every historical figure who crossed Philip's path felt like they were meant to be there and not forced there by the author to give us a recognizable name.<br><br>The fact that he developed relationships with all of these people and it never felt cheesy is a testament to Jakes' writing, considering the rough examples of this exact tactic I've read in other books. The best example would have to be Louisa Morgan's A History of Witches where one of the characters joins a coven run by the Queen of England in World War II to......stop the war...with magic? It was one of the roughest inclusions of a real historical figure I've ever read, and the last one I read before picking up The Bastard, so I was clenched and ready for disappointment. Thankfully, Philip and his complete dumb luck won me over and getting to see these powerhouse names through the eyes of a character who feels real and relatable was a lot of fun for a 1770s America history buff!<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1494480430ra/22718625.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So.....going through the Goodreads reviews of this book, I'm starting to feel like I might be the youngest person alive at this exact moment who has read this. I can not find a single review written by anyone my own age. So, that being said, if you have read this book, I would like to take this time to yell.<br><br>Hold on to your tricorns, everyone.<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><b>Characters:</b><br><br><b>Phillipe/Philip:</b> Soft boy. Needs to let himself ugly cry more. Is having a rough time. Is open to new ideas, but needs to calm down about wanting to strangle people all the time. Loves his mommy. Thinks with his dick. The luckiest dumbass to ever walk the planet. Big Idiot Energy. Hates beds.<br><br><b>Marie Charboneau:</b> Hasn't taken a deep breath in thirty years. Will commit any crime in defense of her son. Founded and patented the "If Looks Could Kill" phrase. Gold digger....but with good intentions.<br><br><b>Girard:</b> a completely forgotten and disregarded hero. I hope he and the French tavern are thriving <br><br><b>Roger Amberly:</b> That Guy™. King of the schoolyard bullies. Little bitch. Peak Middle Child Energy. Poster boy for "white boy problems". Relates to Voldemort in the sense that he's tried to kill one kid for years and just simply cannot accomplish it.<br><br><b>Lady Jane:</b> If you've ever seen the 2005 adaption of Pride and Prejudice, she embodies this hoe<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979615.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Alicia:</b> A snake with nice hair<br><br><b>Mr. Fox:</b> Overly trusting legend. Probably hides a different fugitive in his barn once a week. I almost forgot about him and honestly, that's grounds for arrest.<br><br><b>The Sholtos:</b> The Kindest souls on the planet. The overly exited English family that took in a foreign exchange student and won't stop feeding him. Legends on the sole fact that none of them died.<br><br><b>Sons of Liberty:</b> I would like to thank them for their service in letting Philip stand in the corner during their meetings so his narrative coincided nicely with the growing American conflict. They enjoy talking about their little Newspaper Boy's escapades with a certain lawyer's daughter. Gossip Kings<br><br><b>Anne Ware:</b> QUEEN. Would fight you for looking at her or her man wrong. Rejected a British soldier to his face. Told her father she dates who she wants. Talks openly about her feelings and about how she hopes women will have a better chance in the new world. None of these are insights, just facts, because all I have to say about Anne is that she's perfect and I will be right next to her father about to beat Philip senseless if he hurts her.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979616.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Plot and Pacing:</b><br><br>This plot was a <i>rollercoaster.</i> The moment I started to think that events were slowing down and maybe Philip could breath, NOPE.<br><br>Please enjoy the periodic updates from my Goodreads account while I read this novel:<br><br>31%: Action and sex and action and sex and "breeches" and action and sex.<br><br>41%: Every time I open this book its just me patiently waiting through long monologues about politics for Phillipe to get the shit beaten out of him again<br><br>51%: New place? Sleeping on the streets and dying slowly from fever? No worries! Simply find someone to pick a fight with and some supernaturally kind strange WILL come help you and provide you with everything you need. A Survival Guide by Philip Kent<br><br>68%: Philip finally had sex in a bed and I am so proud of him<br><br>73%: this author, while writing this damn book: "Almost done the chapter...hmmm..it feels like something is missing.....I KNOW" *whacks Philip upside the head with a rock*<br><br>82%: Alright....you didn't have to go THAT HARD!?!?<br><br>90%: "haha....HAHAHA......SHUT UP" - Philip every 5 seconds<br><br>100%: lol, ow <br><br>I did not expect to become so invested in this story or this character. I ran to ThriftBooks as soon as I finished the last chapter this morning and ordered the sequel, I have to know what happens next.<br><br>This story was beautifully crafted. Very, VERY, well researched. And it threw me on a ride of emotional investment in both the historical story and the personal story. This book was able to take me to a time that seems so long ago that it might as well be alien and bring it down to the basic human emotions that force me to relate to characters who would have lived in that alien world.<br><br><b>Conclusion:</b><br><br>All in all, I'm so sorry this review is so long and ridiculous. I loved this book, however I'm glad to angrily point out everything wrong with it and the cringey scenes that are just #NotOkay. Still, I'm so glad my mother forced this on me from her 1970s collection. I am also happy to probably be the only 20-something to write an official review for this.....and if my other 20-somethings are out there, please come talk to me cause I want to DISCUSS!<br><br>Stay tuned for book two, The Rebels, which I am patiently awaiting to arrive in the mail, and I look forward to dumping as many Hamilton gifs into my next review as humanly possible. Let's go fight some British!<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1565639041ra/27979617.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781453255902" href="/book/show/15813339-the-bastard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=my-ebooks">my-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5654692-tim-the-enchanter?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer541060059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2879009495681770029">There was a level of simplicity in the story that disappointed me. I enjoy an epic feeling in historical fiction. Ultimately, I cannot fault the simplicity of the story as the main character of the story is himself a simple man. I suppose I can't be too harsh as I stayed awake until 1 am to finish the book. <br/><br/>I often found the supporting characters more interesting than the main character. The entirety of the first book is a journey of the main character to determine who he wants to be and who he </span>
  <span id="freeText2879009495681770029" style="display:none">There was a level of simplicity in the story that disappointed me. I enjoy an epic feeling in historical fiction. Ultimately, I cannot fault the simplicity of the story as the main character of the story is himself a simple man. I suppose I can't be too harsh as I stayed awake until 1 am to finish the book. <br /><br />I often found the supporting characters more interesting than the main character. The entirety of the first book is a journey of the main character to determine who he wants to be and who he is. This coupled with multiple serendipitous moments that lead to story progression was also disappointing.<br /><br />On the other hand, like any good historical fiction, you are made to feel as if you are looking into another period of time. The elements of the story and the historical setting feel natural. As a Canadian, my knowledge of American History is limited. Although I recognized several supporting characters, i.e. Benjamin Franklin, Samuel Adams, Ben Revere, I am oblivious as to whether the elements of the story accurately depict the historical events. Regardless of this, you leave the story feelings as if the events truly took place.<br /><br />Overall, the book was enjoyable and I look forward to continuing the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2879009495681770029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_83241196" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83241196" />
    <a title="Brent Soderstrum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1356105-brent-soderstrum"><img alt="Brent Soderstrum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277826295p2/1356105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/83241196?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brent Soderstrum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brent Soderstrum" href="/user/show/1356105-brent-soderstrum">Brent Soderstrum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer83241196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11422553964037776780">The first of an 8 volume set of historical fiction by John Jakes. The Kent Family Chronicles done around the bi-centenial covers the Kent family from the 1770s to the 1970s. The Bastard starts out with Phillipe living with his mother Marie in France. His mother tells him that he is the bastard son of an English noblemen. Marie and Phillipe travel to England to insure Phillipe will inherit part of his father's estate. When they arrive they find out his father is dead and his half brother Roger wa</span>
  <span id="freeText11422553964037776780" style="display:none">The first of an 8 volume set of historical fiction by John Jakes. The Kent Family Chronicles done around the bi-centenial covers the Kent family from the 1770s to the 1970s. The Bastard starts out with Phillipe living with his mother Marie in France. His mother tells him that he is the bastard son of an English noblemen. Marie and Phillipe travel to England to insure Phillipe will inherit part of his father's estate. When they arrive they find out his father is dead and his half brother Roger wants Phillipe eliminated.<br /><br />Phillipe and his mother Marie travel to America to avoid being killed by Roger and his henchmen. Marie dies in transit and Phillipe Charbanou changes his name to Phil Kent. <br /><br />In America he has interactions with many famous revolutionary figures including Paul Revere and Ben Franklin. He participates in the Boston Tea Party and first battle of the Revolutionary War. <br /><br />I am a history buff and I enjoy Jake's manner of weaving a fictional story about the Kent family into the history of our country. It gives you an "eye witness" view on important parts of our country's history. I am looking forward to reading the next volume.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11422553964037776780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1633665120374754828">Another book I sort of "stumbled" into. I like history and some historical fiction. This one and the couple of its sequels I read treaded dangerously close to what I would call "soap opera" status.<br/><br/>We follow Philip from the time in his "youth" when he discovers (from his mother who has kept the secret till this point) that he is the "bastard" of the title.   <span id="freeText1633665120374754828" style="display:none">Another book I sort of "stumbled" into. I like history and some historical fiction. This one and the couple of its sequels I read treaded dangerously close to what I would call "soap opera" status.<br /><br />We follow Philip from the time in his "youth" when he discovers (from his mother who has kept the secret till this point) that he is the "bastard" of the title. He is the unacknowledged son of James Amberly, the 6th Duke of Kent. Cool...except that the duke's family isn't thrilled about his existence.<br /><br />Life is tough (I mean really, really) tough for Philip and his mother. We follow them through the trials and tribulations of survival. Finally they cross the ocean to the Colonies where eventually some well known names get drawn into the story, Philip deals with a love triangle (or rectangle or some other geometric form) finally finding love and joining the American revelation.<br /><br />Not a bad read and it sold a "bunch". It was just a little more involved with our character's love "lives" than I usually care for.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1606111165614624226">Well, I figured this would be as much as it is - this falls under Historical, but it should be more of Historical Romance, which is not my fare - Sure, it is no Romance novel with "breasts heaving" kind of thing, but the "hero" is not a very heroic gentleman at all, nor is he a character that I like. No character is one that I like, and that is what made this hard to continue with.<br/><br/>The overview of this novel, and the entire series, sounds promising and interesting, but the words that fell onto th</span>
  <span id="freeText1606111165614624226" style="display:none">Well, I figured this would be as much as it is - this falls under Historical, but it should be more of Historical Romance, which is not my fare - Sure, it is no Romance novel with "breasts heaving" kind of thing, but the "hero" is not a very heroic gentleman at all, nor is he a character that I like. No character is one that I like, and that is what made this hard to continue with.<br /><br />The overview of this novel, and the entire series, sounds promising and interesting, but the words that fell onto these pages was far from interesting. I worked my way through the first 'book' within the novel, and the events were scattered though a through-line existed, if only to move the character to the next 'book' within. <br /><br />Every word and every page seemed to force the character forward instead of him going that way naturally. Even trying to display resilience, you end up just showing him as ill-tempered and stubborn. <br /><br />But, I did get what was promised, eventually, filled with pages of loveless romance and more arguments between the hero and a woman than he with anyone else.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer74221013" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16900695548787174223">Philippe Charbonneau, the illegitimate son of the Duke of Kentland, finds out about his heritage from his mother, Marie, when his father is on his deathbed. Being denied his inheritance by his father's wife and his half-brother, he ends up being a hunted man. To escape the wrath of the Dutchess of Kentland, he flees to American and arrives just before the American Revolution.<br/><br/>I love historical fiction, and John Jakes has a way with transporting you back to the time of all the turmoil and upheaval</span>
  <span id="freeText16900695548787174223" style="display:none">Philippe Charbonneau, the illegitimate son of the Duke of Kentland, finds out about his heritage from his mother, Marie, when his father is on his deathbed. Being denied his inheritance by his father's wife and his half-brother, he ends up being a hunted man. To escape the wrath of the Dutchess of Kentland, he flees to American and arrives just before the American Revolution.<br /><br />I love historical fiction, and John Jakes has a way with transporting you back to the time of all the turmoil and upheaval just before the Revolution. He gives glimpses and accounts of people such as Benjamin Franklin and Samuel Adams and brings such realism to the thoughts and fears of those involved with the pending uprising. This is a well researched book that deserves more than 4 stars. I would have rated it as 5, but I felt the sex scenes were more thrown in for a draw to the female audience and to me took away from the book instead of adding to it. With that being said, it is a great read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16900695548787174223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3323282723536913482">When my septuagenarian coworker recommended this series that came out before I was born, I was skeptical, never having heard of it or its author.   <span id="freeText3323282723536913482" style="display:none">When my septuagenarian coworker recommended this series that came out before I was born, I was skeptical, never having heard of it or its author. Seeing as she also recommended Outlander to me, which I love, I gave The Bastard a shot. Boy, am I glad I did. I am ashamed to say that once I studied American Revolutionary War history in elementary school, I have since hardly given it a passing thought--until I read this book. It puts the blood, sweat, and tears of the people who worked tirelessly to build the foundation of the United States into perspective, and I look forward to reading the next installment in the saga. </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_133430'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133430.Centennial"><img alt="Centennial" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348125094l/133430._SY160_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BBwHbaE1UvyAhpeFhUCbfHRyEo7e1XiwuIGIHcz7ugydWw6RhPFiXBNpJ8IwFG//vEOzqfRK7dQ071xJ+qQ5pA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Michener brings Hawaii’s epic history vividly to life in a classic saga that has captivated readers since its initial publication in 1959. As the volcanic Hawaiian Islands sprout from the ocean floor, the land remains untouched for centuries—until, little more than a thousand years ago, Polynesian seafarers make the perilous journey across the Pacific, flourishing in this tropical paradise according to their ancient traditions. Then, in the early nineteenth century, American missionaries arrive, bringing with them a new creed and a new way of life. Based on exhaustive research and told in Michener’s immersive prose, Hawaii is the story of disparate peoples struggling to keep their identity, live in harmony, and, ultimately, join A classic gothic romance, the story features an 18-year-old Miranda Wells who falls under the spell of a mysterious old mansion and its equally fascinating master. Tired of churning butter, weeding the garden patch, and receiving the dull young farmers who seek her hand in marriage, Miranda is excited by an invitation from the upstate New York estate of her distant relative, the intriguing Nicholas Van Ryn. Her passion is kindled by the icy fire of Nicholas, the last of the Van Ryns, and the luxury of Dragonwyck, and a way of life of which she has only dreamed. Dressed in satin and lace, she becomes part of Dragonwyck, with its Gothic towers, flowering gardens, acres of tenant farms, and dark, terrible secrets. This compelling novel paints a marvelous portrait of a country torn between freedom and feudal traditions; a country divided between the very wealthy and the very poor. Poor tenant farmers at Dragonwyck, the European royalty who visit, and American icons such as Edgar Allan Poe, Herman Melville, and the Astors are vividly brought to life.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5768.John_Jakes">About John Jakes</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6197747487776901109"><b>John William Jakes</b>, the author of more than a dozen novels, is regarded as one of today’s most distinguished writers of historical fiction. His work includes the highly acclaimed Kent Family Chronicles series and the North and South Trilogy. Jakes’s commitment to historical accuracy and evocative storytelling earned him the title of “the godfather of historical novelists” from the Los Angeles Time</span>
  <span id="freeText6197747487776901109" style="display:none"><b>John William Jakes</b>, the author of more than a dozen novels, is regarded as one of today’s most distinguished writers of historical fiction. His work includes the highly acclaimed Kent Family Chronicles series and the North and South Trilogy. Jakes’s commitment to historical accuracy and evocative storytelling earned him the title of “the godfather of historical novelists” from the Los Angeles Times and led to a streak of sixteen consecutive New York Times bestsellers. Jakes has received several awards for his work and is a member of the Authors Guild and the PEN American Center. He and his wife, Rachel, live on the west coast of Florida.<br /><br />Also writes under pseudonyms <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1396149._Jay_Scotland" title=" Jay Scotland" rel="nofollow noopener"> Jay Scotland</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2117002.Alan_Payne" title="Alan Payne" rel="nofollow noopener">Alan Payne</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14173062.Rachel_Ann_Payne" title="Rachel Ann Payne" rel="nofollow noopener">Rachel Ann Payne</a>, Robert Hart Davis, Darius John Granger, John Lee Gray. Has ghost written as William Ard.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6197747487776901109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
